<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Silicon</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Silicon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Silicon (disambiguation)">Silicon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with the silicon-containing synthetic polymer <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Element 14" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Element_14_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Element 14 (disambiguation)">Element 14 (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_silicon" title="Template:Infobox silicon"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_silicon" title="Template talk:Infobox silicon"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_silicon&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Silicon</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Si</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 14. It is a hard and brittle crystalline solid with a blue-grey metallic lustre; and it is a <a href="/wiki/Tetravalence" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetravalence">tetravalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a>. It is a member of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">group 14</a> in the periodic table: <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> is above it; and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> are below it. It is relatively unreactive. Because of its large chemical affinity for oxygen, it was not until 1823 that <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" class="mw-redirect" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> was first able to prepare it and characterize it in pure form.  Its melting and boiling points of 1414&#160;°C and 3265&#160;°C respectively are the second-highest among all the metalloids and nonmetals, being only surpassed by boron. Silicon is the eighth most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">common element</a> in the universe by mass, but very rarely occurs as the pure element in the Earth's crust. It is most widely distributed in <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dusts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planetoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetoids">planetoids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> as various forms of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (silica) or <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>. Over 90% of the Earth's crust is composed of <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>, making silicon the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">second most abundant element</a> in the Earth's crust (about 28% by mass) after <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.
</p><p>Most silicon is used commercially without being separated, and often with little processing of the natural minerals. Such use includes industrial construction with <a href="/wiki/Clays" class="mw-redirect" title="Clays">clays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silica_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica sand">silica sand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone">stone</a>. Silicates are used in <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> for <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a>, and mixed with silica sand and <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> for walkways, foundations, and roads. They are also used in whiteware <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, and in traditional <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Soda-lime_glass" class="mw-redirect" title="Soda-lime glass">soda-lime glass</a> and many other specialty <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glasses</a>. Silicon compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> are used as abrasives and components of high-strength ceramics. Silicon is the basis of the widely used synthetic polymers called <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicones</a>.
</p><p>Elemental silicon also has a large impact on the modern world economy. Most free silicon is used in the <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> refining, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>-casting, and fine chemical industries (often to make <a href="/wiki/Fumed_silica" title="Fumed silica">fumed silica</a>). Even more visibly, the relatively small portion of very highly purified elemental silicon used in semiconductor electronics (&lt; 10%) is essential to <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated circuits">integrated circuits</a> — most computers, cell phones, and modern technology depend on it.
</p><p>Silicon is an essential element in biology, although only traces are required by animals. However, various <a href="/wiki/Sea_sponges" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea sponges">sea sponges</a> and microorganisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Diatoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatoms">diatoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiolaria" title="Radiolaria">radiolaria</a>, secrete skeletal structures made of silica. Silica is deposited in many plant tissues.<sup id="cite_ref-Cutter_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cutter-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/200px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg" width="200" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/300px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/400px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg 2x" data-file-width="469" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a>, discoverer of silicon</div></div></div>
<p>In 1787 <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> suspected that <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a> might be an oxide of a fundamental <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">chemical affinity</a> of silicon for oxygen is high enough that he had no means to reduce the oxide and isolate the element.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> After an attempt to isolate silicon in 1808, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Humphry_Davy" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Humphry Davy">Sir Humphry Davy</a> proposed the name "silicium" for silicon, from the Latin <i>silex</i>, <i>silicis</i> for flint, and adding the "-ium" ending because he believed it to be a metal.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Most other languages use transliterated forms of Davy's name, sometimes adapted to local phonology (e.g. <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Silizium</i>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> <i>silisyum</i>). A few others use instead a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of the Latin root (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>кремний</i>, from <i>кремень</i> "flint"; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>πυριτιο</i> from <i>πυρ</i> "fire"; <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> <i>pii</i> from <i>piikivi</i> "flint").<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1811, <a href="/wiki/Gay-Lussac" class="mw-redirect" title="Gay-Lussac">Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Thénard</a> are thought to have prepared impure <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a>, through the heating of recently isolated <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> metal with <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">silicon tetrafluoride</a>, but they did not purify and characterize the product, nor identify it as a new element.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Silicon was given its present name in 1817 by Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Thomson_(chemist)" title="Thomas Thomson (chemist)">Thomas Thomson</a>. He retained part of Davy's name but added "-on" because he believed that silicon was a <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetal</a> similar to <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In 1823, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> prepared amorphous silicon using approximately the same method as Gay-Lussac (reducing potassium fluorosilicate with molten potassium metal), but purifying the product to a brown powder by repeatedly washing it.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As a result, he is usually given credit for the element's discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The same year, Berzelius became the first to prepare <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrachloride" title="Silicon tetrachloride">silicon tetrachloride</a>; <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">silicon tetrafluoride</a> had already been prepared long before in 1771 by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> by dissolving silica in <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon in its more common crystalline form was not prepared until 31 years later, by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Deville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> By <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolyzing</a> a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> containing approximately 10% silicon, he was able to obtain a slightly impure <a href="/wiki/Allotrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a> of silicon in 1854.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Later, more cost-effective methods have been developed to isolate several allotrope forms, the most recent being <a href="/wiki/Silicene" title="Silicene">silicene</a> in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, research on the chemistry of silicon continued; <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> discovered the first volatile hydrides of silicon, synthesising <a href="/wiki/Trichlorosilane" title="Trichlorosilane">trichlorosilane</a> in 1857 and <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">silane</a> itself in 1858, but a detailed investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Silanes" title="Silanes">silanes</a> was only carried out in the early 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stock" title="Alfred Stock">Alfred Stock</a>, despite early speculation on the matter dating as far back as the beginnings of synthetic organic chemistry in the 1830s.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the first <a href="/wiki/Organosilicon_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organosilicon compound">organosilicon compound</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tetraethylsilane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tetraethylsilane (page does not exist)">tetraethylsilane</a>, was synthesised by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Friedel" title="Charles Friedel">Charles Friedel</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Crafts" title="James Crafts">James Crafts</a> in 1863, but detailed characterisation of organosilicon chemistry was only done in the early 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Kipping" title="Frederic Kipping">Frederic Kipping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting in the 1920s, the work of <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="William Lawrence Bragg">William Lawrence Bragg</a> on <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> successfully elucidated the compositions of the silicates, which had previously been known from <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">analytical chemistry</a> but had not yet been understood, together with <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>'s development of <a href="/wiki/Crystal_chemistry" title="Crystal chemistry">crystal chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Victor_Goldschmidt" title="Victor Goldschmidt">Victor Goldschmidt</a>'s development of <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemistry</a>. The middle of the 20th century saw the development of the chemistry and industrial use of <a href="/wiki/Siloxane" title="Siloxane">siloxanes</a> and the growing use of <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elastomer" title="Elastomer">elastomers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>. In the late 20th century, the complexity of the crystal chemistry of <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a> was mapped, along with the solid-state chemistry of <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">doped</a> <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because silicon is an important element in high-technology semiconductor devices, many places in the world bear its name. For example, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_Valley" title="Santa Clara Valley">Santa Clara Valley</a> in California acquired the nickname <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>, as the element is the base material in the semiconductor industry there. Since then, many other places have been dubbed similarly, including <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Forest" title="Silicon Forest">Silicon Forest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Hills" title="Silicon Hills">Silicon Hills</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Slopes" title="Silicon Slopes">Silicon Slopes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City,_Utah" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt Lake City, Utah">Salt Lake City, Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Saxony" title="Silicon Saxony">Silicon Saxony</a> in Germany, <a href="/wiki/Bangalore#Economy" title="Bangalore">Silicon Valley</a> in India, <a href="/wiki/Mexicali#Silicon_Border" title="Mexicali">Silicon Border</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexicali,_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexicali, Mexico">Mexicali, Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Fen" title="Silicon Fen">Silicon Fen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge, England">Cambridge, England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Street_Roundabout#Silicon_Roundabout" title="Old Street Roundabout">Silicon Roundabout</a> in London, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Glen" title="Silicon Glen">Silicon Glen</a> in Scotland, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Gorge" title="Silicon Gorge">Silicon Gorge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bristol,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Bristol, England">Bristol, England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_and_atomic">Physical and atomic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/170px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png" width="170" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/255px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/340px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon crystallizes in a diamond cubic crystal structure</div></div></div>
<p>A silicon atom has fourteen <a href="/wiki/Electrons" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrons">electrons</a>. In the ground state, they are arranged in the electron configuration [Ne]3s<sup>2</sup>3p<sup>2</sup>. Of these, four are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>, occupying the 3s orbital and two of the 3p orbitals. Like the other members of its group, the lighter <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and the heavier <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, it has the same number of valence electrons as valence orbitals: hence, it can complete its <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet</a> and obtain the stable <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> configuration of <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> by forming <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital hybridization">sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid orbitals</a>, forming tetrahedral SiX<sub>4</sub> derivatives where the central silicon atom shares an electron pair with each of the four atoms it is bonded to.<sup id="cite_ref-King0_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King0-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The first four <a href="/wiki/Ionisation_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionisation energy">ionisation energies</a> of silicon are 786.3, 1576.5, 3228.3, and 4354.4&#160;kJ/mol respectively; these figures are high enough to preclude the possibility of simple cationic chemistry for the element. Following <a href="/wiki/Periodic_trend" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodic trend">periodic trends</a>, its single-bond covalent radius of 117.6&#160;pm is intermediate between those of carbon (77.2&#160;pm) and germanium (122.3&#160;pm). The hexacoordinate ionic radius of silicon may be considered to be 40&#160;pm, although this must be taken as a purely notional figure  given the lack of a simple Si<sup>4+</sup> cation in reality.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood372_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood372-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At standard temperature and pressure, silicon is a shiny <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> with a bluish-grey metallic lustre; as typical for semiconductors, its resistivity drops as temperature rises. This arises because silicon has a small energy gap between its highest occupied energy levels (the valence band) and the lowest unoccupied ones (the conduction band). The <a href="/wiki/Fermi_level" title="Fermi level">Fermi level</a> is about halfway between the valence and conduction bands and is the energy at which a state is as likely to be occupied by an electron as not. Hence pure silicon is an insulator at room temperature. However, doping silicon with a <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> introduces one extra electron per dopant and these may then be excited into the conduction band either thermally or photolytically, creating an <a href="/wiki/Extrinsic_semiconductor#N-type_semiconductors" title="Extrinsic semiconductor">n-type semiconductor</a>. Similarly, doping silicon with a <a href="/wiki/Boron_group" title="Boron group">group 13 element</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> results in the introduction of acceptor levels that trap electrons that may be excited from the filled valence band, creating a <a href="/wiki/Extrinsic_semiconductor#P-type_semiconductors" title="Extrinsic semiconductor">p-type semiconductor</a>. Joining n-type silicon to p-type silicon creates a <a href="/wiki/P-n_junction" class="mw-redirect" title="P-n junction">p-n junction</a> with a common Fermi level; electrons flow from n to p, while holes flow from p to n, creating a voltage drop. This p-n junction thus acts as a <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diode</a> that can rectify alternating current that allows current to pass more easily one way than the other. A <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> is an n-p-n junction, with a thin layer of weakly p-type silicon between two n-type regions. Biasing the emitter through a small forward voltage and the collector through a large reverse voltage allows the transistor to act as a <a href="/wiki/Triode" title="Triode">triode</a> amplifier.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood331_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood331-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon crystallises in a giant covalent structure at standard conditions, specifically in a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_cubic" title="Diamond cubic">diamond cubic</a> lattice. It thus has a high melting point of 1414&#160;°C, as a lot of energy is required to break the strong covalent bonds and melt the solid. It is not known to have any allotropes at standard pressure, but several other crystal structures are known at higher pressures. The general trend is one of increasing <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> with pressure, culminating in a <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> allotrope at about 40&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigapascal" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigapascal">gigapascals</a> known as Si–VII (the standard modification being Si–I). Silicon boils at 3265&#160;°C: this, while high, is still lower than the temperature at which its lighter congener <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> sublimes (3642&#160;°C) and silicon similarly has a lower <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat of vaporisation">heat of vaporisation</a> than carbon, consistent with the fact that the Si–Si bond is weaker than the C–C bond.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood331_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood331-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_silicon" title="Isotopes of silicon">Isotopes of silicon</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring silicon is composed of three stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <sup>28</sup>Si (92.23%), <sup>29</sup>Si (4.67%), and <sup>30</sup>Si (3.10%).<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Out of these, only <sup>29</sup>Si is of use in <a href="/wiki/NMR" class="mw-redirect" title="NMR">NMR</a> and <a href="/wiki/EPR_spectroscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="EPR spectroscopy">EPR spectroscopy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> as it is the only one with a nuclear spin (<i>I</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> All three are produced in stars through the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-burning_process" title="Oxygen-burning process">oxygen-burning process</a>, with <sup>28</sup>Si being made as part of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha process</a> and hence the most abundant. The fusion of <sup>28</sup>Si with alpha particles by <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">photodisintegration</a> rearrangement in stars is known as the <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon-burning process</a>; it is the last stage of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> before the rapid collapse and violent explosion of the star in question in a <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">type II supernova</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Twenty <a href="/wiki/Radioisotopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotopes">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, the two stablest being <sup>32</sup>Si with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of about 150&#160;years, and <sup>31</sup>Si with a half-life of 2.62&#160;hours.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> All the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than seven seconds, and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than one tenth of a second.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Silicon does not have any known <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <sup>32</sup>Si undergoes low-energy <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus-32" title="Phosphorus-32"><sup>32</sup>P</a> and then stable <sup>32</sup><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a>. <sup>31</sup>Si may be produced by the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of natural silicon and is thus useful for quantitative analysis; it can be easily detected by its characteristic beta decay to stable <sup>31</sup><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">P</a>, in which the emitted electron carries up to 1.48&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">MeV</a> of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The known isotopes of silicon range in <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> from 22 to 44.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The most common <a href="/wiki/Decay_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> of the isotopes with mass numbers lower than the three stable isotopes is <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>, primarily forming aluminium isotopes (13 protons) as <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The most common decay mode for the heavier unstable isotopes is beta decay, primarily forming phosphorus isotopes (15 protons) as decay products.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span></h2>

<p>Crystalline bulk silicon is rather inert, but becomes more reactive at high temperatures. Like its neighbour aluminium, silicon forms a thin, continuous surface layer of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (SiO<sub>2</sub>) that protects the metal from oxidation. Thus silicon does not measurably react with the air below 900&#160;°C, but formation of the <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_lustre" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitreous lustre">vitreous</a> dioxide rapidly increases between 950&#160;°C and 1160&#160;°C and when 1400&#160;°C is reached, atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> also reacts to give the nitrides SiN and Si<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. Silicon reacts with gaseous <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> at 600&#160;°C and gaseous <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> at 1000&#160;°C. This oxide layer nevertheless does not prevent reaction with the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> attacks silicon vigorously at room temperature, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> does so at about 300&#160;°C, and <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> at about 500&#160;°C. Silicon does not react with most aqueous acids, but is oxidised and fluorinated by a mixture of concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>; it readily dissolves in hot aqueous alkali to form <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>. At high temperatures, silicon also reacts with <a href="/wiki/Alkyl_halide" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkyl halide">alkyl halides</a>; this reaction can be catalysed by <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> to directly synthesise <a href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">organosilicon</a> chlorides as precursors to <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> polymers. Upon melting, silicon becomes extremely reactive, alloying with most metals to form <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a>, and reducing most metal oxides because the <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat of formation">heat of formation</a> of silicon dioxide is so large. As a result, containers for liquid silicon must be made of <a href="/wiki/Refractory" title="Refractory">refractory</a>, unreactive materials such as <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">zirconium dioxide</a> or group 4, 5, and 6 borides.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood331_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood331-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tetrahedral coordination is a major structural motif in silicon chemistry just as it is for carbon chemistry. However, the 3p subshell is rather more diffuse than the 2p subshell and does not hybridise as well with the 3s subshell. As a result, the chemistry of silicon and its heavier congeners shows significant differences from that of carbon,<sup id="cite_ref-Kaupp_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaupp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and thus octahedral coordination is also significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood331_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood331-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of silicon (1.90) is much less than that of carbon (2.55), because the valence electrons of silicon are further from the nucleus than those of carbon and hence experience smaller electrostatic forces of attraction from the nucleus. The poor overlap of 3p orbitals also results in a much lower tendency toward <a href="/wiki/Catenation" title="Catenation">catenation</a> (formation of Si–Si bonds) for silicon than for carbon, due to the concomitant weakening of the Si–Si bond compared to the C–C bond:<sup id="cite_ref-King43_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King43-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> the average Si–Si bond energy is approximately 226&#160;kJ/mol, compared to a value of 356&#160;kJ/mol for the C–C bond.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood374_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood374-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This results in multiply bonded silicon compounds generally being much less stable than their carbon counterparts, an example of the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond_rule" title="Double bond rule">double bond rule</a>. On the other hand, the presence of 3d orbitals in the valence shell of silicon suggests the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Hypervalence" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypervalence">hypervalence</a>, as seen in five and six-coordinate derivatives of silicon such as <span class="chemf nowrap">SiX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">SiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-King43_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King43-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Lastly, because of the increasing energy gap between the valence s and p orbitals as the group is descended, the divalent state grows in importance from carbon to lead, so that a few unstable divalent compounds are known for silicon; this lowering of the main oxidation state, in tandem with increasing atomic radii, results in an increase of metallic character down the group. Silicon already shows some incipient metallic behavior, particularly in the behavior of its oxide compounds and its reaction with acids as well as bases (though this takes some effort), and is hence often referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> rather than a nonmetal.<sup id="cite_ref-King43_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King43-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> However, metallicity does not become clear in group 14 until germanium and dominant until tin, with the growing importance of the lower +2 oxidation state.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon shows clear differences from carbon. For example, <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> has very few analogies with silicon chemistry, while <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> minerals have a structural complexity unseen in <a href="/wiki/Oxocarbon" title="Oxocarbon">oxocarbons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood328-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Silicon tends to resemble germanium far more than it does carbon, and this resemblance is enhanced by the <a href="/wiki/D-block_contraction" title="D-block contraction">d-block contraction</a>, resulting in the size of the germanium atom being much closer to that of the silicon atom than periodic trends would predict.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood372_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood372-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are still some differences because of the growing importance of the divalent state in germanium compared to silicon, which result in germanium being significantly more metallic than silicon. Additionally, the lower Ge–O bond strength compared to the Si–O bond strength results in the absence of "germanone" polymers that would be analogous to <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> polymers.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood374_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood374-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicides">Silicides</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">Silicide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Binary_compounds_of_silicon" title="Binary compounds of silicon">Binary compounds of silicon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/400px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png" width="400" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/600px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/800px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="427" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram of the Fe–Si system</div></div></div>
<p>Many metal <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a> are known, most of which have formulae that cannot be explained through simple appeals to <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">valence</a>: their bonding ranges from <a href="/wiki/Metallic_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Metallic bond">metallic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionic bond">ionic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a>. Some known stoichiometries are M<sub>6</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si, M<sub>4</sub>Si, M<sub>15</sub>Si<sub>4</sub>, M<sub>3</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>, M<sub>2</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si<sub>3</sub>, M<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>, MSi, M<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>3</sub>, MSi<sub>2</sub>, MSi<sub>3</sub>, and MSi<sub>6</sub>. They are structurally more similar to the <a href="/wiki/Boride" title="Boride">borides</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a>, in keeping with the <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_relationship" title="Diagonal relationship">diagonal relationship</a> between <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and silicon, although the larger size of silicon than boron means that exact structural analogies are few and far between. The heats of formation of the silicides are usually similar to those of the borides and carbides of the same elements, but they usually melt at lower temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Silicides are known for all stable elements in groups 1–10, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>: in particular, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and the transition metals of groups 4–10 show the widest range of stoichiometries. Except for <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, the metals in groups 11–15 do not form silicides. Most instead form <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" class="mw-redirect" title="Eutectic mixture">eutectic mixtures</a>, although the heaviest <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a> <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> are completely immiscible with liquid silicon.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicides are usually prepared by direct reaction of the elements. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a> react with silicon or silicon oxide to give silicides. Nevertheless, even with these highly electropositive elements true silicon anions are not obtainable, and most of these compounds are semiconductors. For example, the alkali metal silicides <span class="chemf nowrap">(M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(E<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)</span> contain pyramidal tricoordinate silicon in the <span class="chemf nowrap">Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion, isoeelctronic with <a href="/wiki/White_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="White phosphorus">white phosphorus</a>, P<sub>4</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-King45_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King45-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Metal-rich silicides tend to have isolated silicon atoms (e. g. Cu<sub>5</sub>Si); with increasing silicon content, catenation increases, resulting in isolated clusters of two (e. g. U<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>) or four silicon atoms (e. g. [K<sup>+</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[Si<sub>4</sub>]<sup>4−</sup>) at first, followed by chains (e. g. CaSi), layers (e. g. CaSi<sub>2</sub>), or three-dimensional networks of silicon atoms spanning space (e. g. α-ThSi<sub>2</sub>) as the silicon content rises even higher.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The silicides of the group 1 and 2 metals are usually more reactive than the transition metal silicides. The latter usually do not react with aqueous reagents, except for <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>; however, they do react with much more aggressive reagents such as liquid <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, or gaseous fluorine or <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> when red-hot. The pre-transition metal silicides instead readily react with water and aqueous acids, usually producing hydrogen or silanes:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Na<sub>2</sub>Si + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>Mg<sub>2</sub>Si + 2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → 2 MgSO<sub>4</sub> + SiH<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Products often vary with the stoichiometry of the silicide reactant. For example, Ca<sub>2</sub>Si is polar and non-conducting and has the anti-PbCl<sub>2</sub> structure with single isolated silicon atoms, and reacts with water to produce <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a>, hydrated silicon dioxide, and hydrogen gas. CaSi with its zigzag chains of silicon atoms instead reacts to give silanes and polymeric SiH<sub>2</sub>, while CaSi<sub>2</sub> with its puckered layers of silicon atoms does not react with water, but will react with dilute hydrochloric acid: the product is a yellow polymeric solid with stoichiometry Si<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood335_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood335-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silanes">Silanes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silanes" title="Silanes">Silanes</a></div>
<p>Speculation on silicon hydride chemistry started in the 1830s, contemporary with the development of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">Silane</a> itself, as well as <a href="/wiki/Trichlorosilane" title="Trichlorosilane">trichlorosilane</a>, were first synthesised by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heinrich_Buff&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Heinrich Buff (page does not exist)">Heinrich Buff</a> in 1857 by reacting aluminium–silicon alloys with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, and characterised as SiH<sub>4</sub> and SiHCl<sub>3</sub> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Friedel" title="Charles Friedel">Charles Friedel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ladenburg" title="Albert Ladenburg">Albert Ladenburg</a> in 1867. <a href="/wiki/Disilane" title="Disilane">Disilane</a> (Si<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>) followed in 1902, when it was first made by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Moissan" title="Henri Moissan">Henri Moissan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Smiles" title="Samuel Smiles">Samuel Smiles</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Protonolysis" title="Protonolysis">protonolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_silicide" title="Magnesium silicide">magnesium silicides</a>. Further investigation had to wait until 1916 because of the great reactivity and thermal instability of the silanes; it was then that <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stock" title="Alfred Stock">Alfred Stock</a> began to study silicon hydrides in earnest with new greaseless vacuum techniques, as they were found as contaminants of his focus, the <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">boron hydrides</a>. The names <i>silanes</i> and <i>boranes</i> are his, based on analogy with the <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Moissan and Smiles' method of preparation of silanes and silane derivatives via protonolysis of metal silicides is still used, although the yield is lowered by the hydrolysis of the products that occurs simultaneously, so that the preferred route today is to treat substituted silanes with hydride reducing agents such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a> in etheric solutions at low temperatures. Direct reaction of HX or RX with silicon, possibly with a catalyst such as copper, is also a viable method of producing substituted silanes.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The silanes comprise a <a href="/wiki/Homologous_series" title="Homologous series">homologous series</a> of silicon hydrides with a general formula of Si<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>&#160;+&#160;2</sub>. They are all strong <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agents</a>. Unbranched and branched trains are known up to <i>n</i>=8, and the cycles Si<sub>5</sub>H<sub>10</sub> and Si<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub> are also known. The first two, silane and disilane, are colourless gases; the heavier members of the series are volatile liquids. All silanes are very reactive and catch fire or explode spontaneously in air. They become less thermally stable with room temperature, so that only silane is indefinitely stable at room temperature, although disilane does not decompose very quickly (only 2.5% of a sample decomposes after the passage of eight months).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> They decompose to form polymeric <a href="/wiki/Polysilicon_hydride" title="Polysilicon hydride">polysilicon hydride</a> and hydrogen gas.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> As expected from the difference in atomic weight, the silanes are less volatile than the corresponding alkanes and boranes, but more so than the corresponding germanes. They are much more reactive than the corresponding alkanes, because of the larger radius of silicon compared to carbon facilitating <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophilic attack</a> at the silicon, the greater polarity of the Si–H bond compared to the C–H bond, and the ability of silicon to expand its octet and hence form adducts and lower the reaction's <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silane <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> gives polymeric species and finally elemental silicon and hydrogen; indeed ultrapure silicon is commercially produced by the pyrolysis of silane. While the thermal decomposition of alkanes starts by the breaking of a C–H or C–C bond and the formation of radical intermediates, polysilanes decompose by eliminating <a href="/wiki/Silene" title="Silene">silenes</a> :SiH<sub>2</sub> or :SiHR, as the activation energy of this process (~210&#160;kJ/mol) is much less than the Si–Si and Si–H bond energies. While pure silanes do not react with pure water or dilute acids, traces of alkali catalyse immediate hydrolysis to hydrated silicon dioxide. If the reaction is carried out in <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, controlled solvolysis results in the products SiH<sub>2</sub>(OMe)<sub>2</sub>, SiH(OMe)<sub>3</sub>, and Si(OMe)<sub>4</sub>. The Si–H bond also adds to <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a>, a reaction which proceeds slowly and speeds up with increasing substitution of the silane involved. At 450&#160;°C, silane participates in an <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">addition reaction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Ring-opening_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring-opening reaction">ring-opening reaction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>. Direct reaction of the silanes with chlorine or bromine results in explosions at room temperature, but the reaction of silane with bromine at −80&#160;°C is controlled and yields bromosilane and dibromosilane. The monohalosilanes may be formed by reacting silane with the appropriate <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halide</a> with an Al<sub>2</sub>X<sub>6</sub> catalyst, or by reacting silane with a solid <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> halide in a heated flow reactor:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiH<sub>4</sub> + 2 AgCl <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">260&#160;°C</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">&#160;</span></span></span> SiH<sub>3</sub>Cl + HCl + 2 Ag</dd></dl>
<p>Among the derivatives of silane, <a href="/wiki/Iodosilane" title="Iodosilane">iodosilane</a> (SiH<sub>3</sub>I) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium_silanide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Potassium silanide (page does not exist)">potassium silanide</a> (KSiH<sub>3</sub>) are very useful synthetic intermediates in the production of more complicated silicon-containing compounds: the latter is a colourless crystalline ionic solid containing K<sup>+</sup> cations and <span class="chemf nowrap">SiH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> anions in the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">NaCl</a> structure, and is made by the reduction of silane by <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> metal.<sup id="cite_ref-King47_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King47-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the reactive hypervalent species <span class="chemf nowrap">SiH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> is also known.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood337_23-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood337-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> With suitable organic substituents it is possible to produce stable polysilanes: they have surprisingly high electric conductivities, arising from <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">sigma</a> delocalisation of the electrons in the chain.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span></h3>
<p>Silicon and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> readily react with all four stable halogens, forming the colourless, reactive and volatile silicon tetrahalides.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood340_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood340-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">Silicon tetrafluoride</a> may also be made by fluorinating the other silicon halides, and is produced by the attack of <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> on glass.<sup id="cite_ref-King48_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King48-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Heating two different tetrahalides together also produce a random mixture of mixed halides, which may also be produced by halogen exchange reactions. The melting and boiling points of these species usually rise with increasing atomic weight, though there are many exceptions: for example, the melting and boiling points drop as one passes from SiFBr<sub>3</sub> through SiFClBr<sub>2</sub> to SiFCl<sub>2</sub>Br. The shift from the hypoelectronic elements in group 13 and earlier to the group 14 elements is illustrated by the change from an infinite ionic structure in <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_fluoride" title="Aluminium fluoride">aluminium fluoride</a> to a lattice of simple covalent silicon tetrafluoride molecules, as dictated by the lower electronegativity of aluminium than silicon, the stoichiometry (the +4 oxidation state being too high for true ionicity), and the smaller size of the silicon atom compared to the aluminium atom.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood340_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood340-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrachloride" title="Silicon tetrachloride">Silicon tetrachloride</a> is manufactured on a huge scale as a precursor to the production of pure silicon, silicon dioxide, and some silicon <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood340_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood340-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The silicon tetrahalides hydrolyse readily in water, unlike the carbon tetrahalides, again because of the larger size of the silicon atom rendering it more open to nucleophilic attack and the ability of the silicon atom to expand its octet which carbon lacks.<sup id="cite_ref-King48_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King48-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The reaction of silicon fluoride with excess <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> produces the octahedral <a href="/wiki/Hexafluorosilicic_acid" title="Hexafluorosilicic acid">hexafluorosilicate</a> anion <span class="chemf nowrap">SiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-King48_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King48-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Analogous to the silanes, halopolysilanes Si<sub><i>n</i></sub>X<sub>2<i>n</i>&#160;+&#160;2</sub> are also known. While catenation in carbon compounds is maximised in the hydrogen compounds rather than the halides, the opposite is true for silicon, so that the halopolysilanes are known up to at least Si<sub>14</sub>F<sub>30</sub>, Si<sub>6</sub>Cl<sub>14</sub>, and Si<sub>4</sub>Br<sub>10</sub>. A suggested explanation for this phenomenon is the compensation for the electron loss of silicon to the more electronegative halogen atoms by <a href="/wiki/Pi_backbonding" title="Pi backbonding">pi backbonding</a> from the filled p<sub>π</sub> orbitals on the halogen atoms to the empty d<sub>π</sub> orbitals on silicon: this is similar to the situation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> in <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal carbonyl complex">metal carbonyl complexes</a> and explains their stability. These halopolysilanes may be produced by <a href="/wiki/Comproportionation" title="Comproportionation">comproportionation</a> of silicon tetrahalides with elemental silicon, or by condensation of lighter halopolysilanes (<a href="/wiki/Trimethylammonium" class="mw-redirect" title="Trimethylammonium">trimethylammonium</a> being a useful catalyst for this reaction).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood340_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood340-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silica">Silica</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">Silicon dioxide</a> (SiO<sub>2</sub>), also known as silica, is one of the best-studied compounds, second only to <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a>. Twelve different crystal modifications of silica are known, the most common being α-<a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, a major constituent of many rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>. It is also known to occur pure as <a href="/wiki/Rock_crystal" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock crystal">rock crystal</a>; impure forms are known as <a href="/wiki/Rose_quartz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rose quartz">rose quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoky_quartz" title="Smoky quartz">smoky quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morion_(mineral)" class="mw-redirect" title="Morion (mineral)">morion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amethyst" title="Amethyst">amethyst</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citrine" class="mw-redirect" title="Citrine">citrine</a>. Some poorly crystalline forms of quartz are also known, such as <a href="/wiki/Chalcedony" title="Chalcedony">chalcedony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrysoprase" title="Chrysoprase">chrysoprase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agate" title="Agate">agate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onyx" title="Onyx">onyx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jasper" title="Jasper">jasper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heliotrope_(mineral)" title="Heliotrope (mineral)">heliotrope</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a>. Other modifications of silicon dioxide are known in some other minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Tridymite" title="Tridymite">tridymite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cristobalite" title="Cristobalite">cristobalite</a>, as well as the much less common <a href="/wiki/Coesite" title="Coesite">coesite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stishovite" title="Stishovite">stishovite</a>. Biologically generated forms are also known as <a href="/wiki/Kieselguhr" class="mw-redirect" title="Kieselguhr">kieselguhr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vitrification" title="Vitrification">Vitreous</a> silicon dioxide is known as <a href="/wiki/Tektite" title="Tektite">tektites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a>, and rarely as <a href="/wiki/Lechatelierite" title="Lechatelierite">lechatelierite</a>. Some synthetic forms are known as <a href="/wiki/Keatite" title="Keatite">keatite</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=W-silica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="W-silica (page does not exist)">W-silica</a>. <a href="/wiki/Opal" title="Opal">Opals</a> are composed of complicated crystalline aggregates of partially hydrated silicon dioxide.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood342_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood342-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Lechatelierite
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Most crystalline forms of silica are made of infinite arrangements of {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra (with Si at the center) connected at their corners, with each oxygen atom linked to two silicon atoms. In the thermodynamically stable room-temperature form, α-quartz, these tetrahedra are linked in intertwined helical chains with two different Si–O distances (159.7 and 161.7&#160;pm) with a Si–O–Si angle of 144°. These helices can be either left- or right-handed, so that individual α-quartz crystals are optically active. At 537&#160;°C, this transforms quickly and reversibly into the similar β-quartz, with a change of the Si–O–Si angle to 155° but a retention of handedness. Further heating to 867&#160;°C results in another reversible phase transition to β-tridymite, in which some Si–O bonds are broken to allow for the arrangement of the {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra into a more open and less dense hexagonal structure. This transition is slow and hence tridymite occurs as a metastable mineral even below this transition temperature; when cooled to about 120&#160;°C it quickly and reversibly transforms by slight displacements of individual silicon and oxygen atoms to α-tridymite, similarly to the transition from α-quartz to β-quartz. β-tridymite slowly transforms to cubic β-cristobalite at about 1470&#160;°C, which once again exists metastably below this transition temperature and transforms at 200–280&#160;°C to α-cristobalite via small atomic displacements. β-cristobalite melts at 1713&#160;°C; the freezing of silica from the melt is quite slow and <a href="/wiki/Vitrification" title="Vitrification">vitrification</a>, or the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, is likely to occur instead. In vitreous silica, the {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra remain corner-connected, but the symmetry and periodicity of the crystalline forms are lost. Because of the slow conversions between these three forms, it is possible upon rapid heating to melt β-quartz (1550&#160;°C) or β-tridymite (1703&#160;°C). Silica boils at approximately 2800&#160;°C. Other high-pressure forms of silica are known, such as coesite and stishovite: these are known in nature, formed under the shock pressure of a meteorite impact and then rapidly quenched to preserve the crystal structure. Similar melting and cooling of silica occurs following <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> strikes, forming glassy <a href="/wiki/Lechatelierite" title="Lechatelierite">lechatelierite</a>. W-silica is an unstable low-density form involving {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra sharing opposite edges instead of corners, forming parallel chains similarly to <a href="/wiki/Silicon_disulfide" title="Silicon disulfide">silicon disulfide</a> (SiS<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon_diselenide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Silicon diselenide (page does not exist)">silicon diselenide</a> (SiSe<sub>2</sub>): it quickly returns to forming amorphous silica with heat or traces of water.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood342_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood342-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/220px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/330px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/440px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Condensed polysilicic acid</div></div></div>
<p>Silica is rather inert chemically. It is not attacked by any acids other than hydrofluoric acid. However, it slowly dissolves in hot concentrated alkalis, and does so rather quickly in fused metal hydroxides or carbonates, to give metal silicates. Among the elements, it is attacked only by fluorine at room temperature to form silicon tetrafluoride: hydrogen and carbon also react, but require temperatures over 1000&#160;°C to do so. Silica nevertheless reacts with many metal and <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> oxides to form a wide variety of compounds important in the glass and ceramic industries above all, but also have many other uses: for example, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_silicate" title="Sodium silicate">sodium silicate</a> is often used in detergents due to its <a href="/wiki/Buffer_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Buffer (chemistry)">buffering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponifying</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emulsification" class="mw-redirect" title="Emulsification">emulsifying</a> properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood342_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood342-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicic_acids">Silicic acids</span></h3>
<p>Adding water to silica drops its melting point by around 800&#160;°C due to the breaking of the structure by replacing Si–O–Si linkages with terminating Si–OH groups. Increasing water concentration results in the formation of hydrated <a href="/wiki/Silica_gel" title="Silica gel">silica gels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal</a> silica dispersions. Many hydrates and <a href="/wiki/Silicic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicic acid">silicic acids</a> exist in the most dilute of aqueous solutions, but these are rather insoluble and quickly precipitate and condense and cross-link to form various polysilicic acids of variable combinations following the formula [SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub>(OH)<sub>4−2<i>x</i></sub>]<sub><i>n</i></sub>, similar to the behaviour of <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, among other elements. Hence, although some simple silicic acids have been identified in dilute solutions, such as <a href="/wiki/Orthosilicic_acid" title="Orthosilicic acid">orthosilicic acid</a> Si(OH)<sub>4</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Metasilicic_acid" title="Metasilicic acid">metasilicic acid</a> SiO(OH)<sub>2</sub>, none of these are likely to exist in the solid state.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood342_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood342-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicate_minerals">Silicate minerals</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">Silicate minerals</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-top:0; margin-left:1em; text-align:center; font-size:10pt; line-height:11pt; width:25%;">
<caption style="margin-bottom: 5px;">Typical coordination of metal cations in silicates (ionic radii in <a href="/wiki/Picometre" title="Picometre">pm</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
