<p>About 95% of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crustal</a> rocks are made of silica or silicate and <a href="/wiki/Aluminosilicate" title="Aluminosilicate">aluminosilicate</a> minerals, as reflected in oxygen, silicon, and aluminium being the three most common elements in the crust (in that order).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Measured by mass, silicon makes up 27.7% of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s crust">Earth's crust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Pure silicon crystals are very rarely found in nature, but notable exceptions are crystals as large as to 0.3&#160;mm across found during sampling gases from the <a href="/wiki/Medvezhya" title="Medvezhya">Kudriavy</a> volcano on <a href="/wiki/Iturup" title="Iturup">Iturup</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KorzhinskyTkachenko1995_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KorzhinskyTkachenko1995-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicate and aluminosilicate minerals have many different structures and varying stoichiometry, but they may be classified following some general principles. Tetrahedral {SiO<sub>4</sub>} units are common to almost all these compounds, either as discrete structures, or combined into larger units by the sharing of corner oxygen atoms. These may be divided into <i>neso</i>-silicates (discrete {SiO<sub>4</sub>} units) sharing no oxygen atoms, <i>soro</i>-silicates (discrete {Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>} units) sharing one, <i>cyclo</i>-silicates (closed ring structures) and <i>ino</i>-silicates (continuous chain or ribbon structures) both sharing two, <i>phyllo</i>-silicates (continuous sheets) sharing three, and <i>tecto</i>-silicates (continuous three-dimensional frameworks) sharing four. The lattice of oxygen atoms that results is usually close-packed or close to it, with the charge being balanced by other cations in various different polyhedral sites according to size.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Orthosilicate" title="Orthosilicate">orthosilicates</a> M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">II</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span> (M = Be, Mg, Mn, Fe, Zn) and ZrSiO<sub>4</sub> are <i>neso</i>-silicates. Be<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Phenacite" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenacite">phenacite</a>) is unusual as both Be<sup>II</sup> and Si<sup>IV</sup> occupy tetrahedral four-coordinated sites; the other divalent cations instead occupy six-coordinated octahedral sites and often isomorphously replace each other as in <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, (Mg,Fe,Mn)<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>. <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">Zircon</a>, ZrSiO<sub>4</sub>, demands eight-coordination of the Zr<sup>IV</sup> cations due to stoichiometry and because of their larger ionic radius (84&#160;pm). Also significant are the <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnets</a>, [M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">II</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">III</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>], in which the divalent cations (e.g. Ca, Mg, Fe) are eight-coordinated and the trivalent ones are six-coordinated (e.g. Al, Cr, Fe). Regular coordination is not always present: for example, it is not found in Ca<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>, which mixes six- and eight-coordinate sites for Ca<sup>II</sup>. <i>Soro</i>-silicates, involving discrete double or triple tetrahedral units, are quite rare: metasilicates involving cyclic "[(SiO<sub>3</sub>)<sub><i>n</i></sub>]<sup>2<i>n</i>−</sup>" units of corner-abutting tetrahedra forming a polygonal ring are also known.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chain metasilicates, {SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>}<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8734;</sub></span>, form by corner-sharing of an indefinite chain of linked {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra. Many differences arise due to the differing repeat distances of conformation across the line of tetrahedra. A repeat distance of two is most common, as in most <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> minerals, but repeat distances of one, three, four, five, six, seven, nine, and twelve are also known. These chains can then link across each other to form double chains and ribbons, as in the <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> minerals, involving repeated chains of cyclic tetrahedron rings.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/220px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/330px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/440px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 2x" data-file-width="1876" data-file-height="1420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical zeolite structure</div></div></div>
<p>Layer silicates, such as the clay minerals and the <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">micas</a>, are very common, and are often formed by horizontal cross-linking of metasilicate chains or planar condensation of smaller units. An example is <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a> [Al<sub>2</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>]; in many of these minerals cation and anion replacement is common, so that for example tetrahedral Si<sup>IV</sup> may be replaced by Al<sup>III</sup>, octahedral Al<sup>III</sup> by Mg<sup>II</sup>, and OH<sup>−</sup> by F<sup>−</sup>. Three-dimensional framework aluminosilicates are structurally very complex; they may be conceived of as starting from the SiO<sub>2</sub> structure, but having replaced up to one-half of the Si<sup>IV</sup> atoms with Al<sup>III</sup>, they require more cations to be included in the structure to balance charge. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a> (the most abundant minerals on the Earth), <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarines</a>. Many feldspars can be thought of as forming part of the ternary system NaAlSi<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>–KAlSi<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>–CaAl<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. Their lattice is destroyed by high pressure prompting Al<sup>III</sup> to undergo six-coordination rather than four-coordination, and this reaction destroying feldspars may be a reason for the <a href="/wiki/Mohorovi%C4%8Di%C4%87_discontinuity" title="Mohorovičić discontinuity">Mohorovičić discontinuity</a>, which would imply that the crust and mantle have the same chemical composition but different lattices, although this is not a universally held view. Zeolites have many polyhedral cavities in their frameworks (<a href="/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron" title="Truncated cuboctahedron">truncated cuboctahedra</a> being most common, but other polyhedra are also known as zeolite cavities), allowing them to include loosely bound molecules such as water in their structure. Ultramarines alternate silicon and aluminium atoms and include a variety of other anions such as Cl<sup>−</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, but are otherwise similar to the feldspars.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood347_46-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood347-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic_compounds">Other inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_disulfide" title="Silicon disulfide">Silicon disulfide</a> (SiS<sub>2</sub>) is formed by burning silicon in gaseous sulfur at 100&#160;°C; sublimation of the resulting compound in nitrogen results in white, flexible long fibers reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> with a structure similar to W-silica. This melts at 1090&#160;°C and sublimes at 1250&#160;°C; at high temperature and pressure this transforms to a crystal structure analogous to cristobalite. However, SiS<sub>2</sub> lacks the variety of structures of SiO<sub>2</sub>, and quickly hydrolyses to silica and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. It is also ammonoloysed quickly and completely by liquid <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> as follows to form an <a href="/wiki/Imide" title="Imide">imide</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood359_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood359-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiS<sub>2</sub> + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → Si(NH)<sub>2</sub> + 2 NH<sub>4</sub>SH</dd></dl>
<p>It reacts with the sulfides of sodium, magnesium, aluminium, and iron to form metal <a href="/wiki/Thiosilicate" title="Thiosilicate">thiosilicates</a>: reaction with <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> results in <a href="/wiki/Tetraethylsilicate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetraethylsilicate">tetraethylsilicate</a> Si(OEt)<sub>4</sub> and hydrogen sulfide. Ethylsilicate is useful as its controlled hydrolysis produces adhesive or film-like forms of silica. Reacting hydrogen sulfide with silicon tetrahalides yields silicon thiohalides such as S(SiCl)<sub>3</sub>, cyclic Cl<sub>2</sub>Si(μ-S)<sub>2</sub>SiCl<sub>2</sub>, and crystalline (SiSCl<sub>2</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. Despite the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond_rule" title="Double bond rule">double bond rule</a>, stable organosilanethiones RR'Si=S have been made thanks to the stabilising mechanism of intermolecular coordination via an <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> group.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood359_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood359-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_nitride" title="Silicon nitride">Silicon nitride</a>, Si<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, can be formed by directly reacting silicon with nitrogen above 1300&#160;°C, but a more economical means of production is by heating silica and coke in a stream of nitrogen and hydrogen gas at 1500&#160;°C. It would make a promising <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> if not for the difficulty of working with and sintering it: it is chemically near-totally inert, and even above 1000&#160;°C it keeps its strength, shape, and continues to be resistant to wear and corrosion. It is very hard (9 on the <a href="/wiki/Mohs_hardness_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohs hardness scale">Mohs hardness scale</a>), dissociates only at 1900&#160;°C at 1&#160;atm, and is quite dense (density 3.185&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>), because of its compact structure similar to that of phenacite (Be<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>). A similar refractory material is Si<sub>2</sub>N<sub>2</sub>O, formed by heating silicon and silica at 1450&#160;°C in an argon stream containing 5% nitrogen gas, involving 4-coordinate silicon and 3-coordinate nitrogen alternating in puckered hexagonal tilings interlinked by non-linear Si–O–Si linkages to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood359_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood359-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reacting silyl halides with ammonia or alkylammonia derivatives in the gaseous phase or in ethanolic solution produces various volatile silylamides, which are silicon analogues of the <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood359_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood359-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 SiH<sub>3</sub>Cl + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 3 NH<sub>4</sub>Cl</dd>
<dd>SiH<sub>3</sub>Br + 2 Me<sub>2</sub>NH → SiH<sub>3</sub>NMe<sub>2</sub> + Me<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub>Br</dd>
<dd>4 SiH<sub>3</sub>I + 5 N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> → (SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>NN(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 4 N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>I</dd></dl>
<p>Many such compounds have been prepared, the only known restriction being that the nitrogen is always tertiary, and species containing the SiH–NH group are unstable at room temperature. The stoichiometry around the nitrogen atom in compounds such as N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>is planar, which has been attributed to a p<sub>π</sub>–d<sub>π</sub> interaction between a lone pair on nitrogen and an empty d<sub>π</sub> orbital on silicon. Similarly, trisilylamines are weaker as ligands than their carbon analogues, the tertiary <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>, although substitution of some SiH<sub>3</sub> groups by CH<sub>3</sub> groups mitigates this weakness. For example, N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> does not form an <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> with <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">BH<sub>3</sub></a> at all, while MeN(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and Me<sub>2</sub>NSiH<sub>3</sub> form adducts at low temperatures that decompose upon warming. Some silicon analogues of <a href="/wiki/Imine" title="Imine">imines</a>, with a Si=N double bond, are known: the first found was Bu<sup><i>t</i></sup><sub>2</sub>Si=N–SiBu<sup><i>t</i></sup><sub>3</sub>, which was discovered in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood359_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood359-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/220px-SiC_p1390066.jpg" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/330px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/440px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1419" data-file-height="1206" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon carbide</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">Silicon carbide</a> (SiC) was first made by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Goodrich_Acheson" title="Edward Goodrich Acheson">Edward Goodrich Acheson</a> in 1891, who named it carborundum to reference its intermediate hardness and abrasive power between <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> (an allotrope of carbon) and <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a>). He soon founded a company to manufacture it, and today about one million tonnes are produced each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood334_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood334-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Silicon carbide exists in about 250 crystalline forms.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The polymorphism of SiC is characterized by a large family of similar crystalline structures called polytypes. They are variations of the same chemical compound that are identical in two dimensions and differ in the third. Thus they can be viewed as layers stacked in a certain sequence.<sup id="cite_ref-jap_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jap-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> It is made industrially by reduction of quartz sand with excess coke or anthracite at 2000–2500&#160;°C in an electric furnace:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood334_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood334-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiO<sub>2</sub> + 2 C → Si + 2 CO</dd>
<dd>Si + C → SiC</dd></dl>
<p>It is the most thermally stable binary silicon compound, only decomposing through loss of silicon starting from around 2700&#160;°C. It is resistant to most aqueous acids, <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a> being an exception. It forms a protective layer of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> on the surface and hence only oxidises appreciably in air above 1000&#160;°C; removal of this layer by molten hydroxides or carbonates leads to quick oxidation. Silicon carbide is rapidly attacked by chlorine gas, which forms SiCl<sub>4</sub> and carbon at 100&#160;°C and SiCl<sub>4</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">CCl<sub>4</sub></a> at 1000&#160;°C. It is mostly used as an abrasive and a refractory material, as it is chemically stable and very strong, and it fractures to form a very sharp cutting edge. It is also useful as an intrinsic semiconductor, as well as an extrinsic semiconductor upon being doped.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood334_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood334-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In its diamond-like behavior it serves as an illustration of the chemical similarity between carbon and silicon.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organosilicon_compounds">Organosilicon compounds</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">Organosilicon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/400px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png" width="400" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/600px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/800px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png 2x" data-file-width="1057" data-file-height="346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hydrosilylation reaction, in which Si–H is added to an unsaturated substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Because the Si–C bond is close in strength to the C–C bond, organosilicon compounds tend to be markedly thermally and chemically stable. For example, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tetraphenylsilane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tetraphenylsilane (page does not exist)">tetraphenylsilane</a> (SiPh<sub>4</sub>) may be distilled in air even at its boiling point of 428&#160;°C, and so can its substituted derivatives Ph<sub>3</sub>SiCl and Ph<sub>2</sub>SiCl<sub>2</sub>, which boil at 378&#160;°C and 305&#160;°C respectively. Furthermore, since carbon and silicon are chemical congeners, organosilicon chemistry shows some significant similarities with carbon chemistry, for example in the propensity of such compounds for catenation and forming multiple bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, significant differences also arise: since silicon is more electropositive than carbon, bonds to more electronegative elements are generally stronger with silicon than with carbon, and vice versa. Thus the Si–F bond is significantly stronger than even the <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93fluorine_bond" title="Carbon–fluorine bond">C–F bond</a> and is one of the strongest single bonds, while the Si–H bond is much weaker than the C–H bond and is readily broken. Furthermore, the ability of silicon to expand its octet is not shared by carbon, and hence some organosilicon reactions have no organic analogues. For example, nucleophilic attack on silicon does not proceed by the <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2</a> or <a href="/wiki/SN1_reaction" title="SN1 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>1</a> processes, but instead goes through a negatively charged true pentacoordinate intermediate and appears like a substitution at a hindered tertiary atom. This works for silicon, unlike for carbon, because the long Si–C bonds reduce the steric hindrance and the d-orbital of silicon is geometrically unconstrained for nucleophilic attack, unlike for example a C–O σ* antibonding orbital. Nevertheless, despite these differences, the mechanism is still often called "S<sub>N</sub>2 at silicon" for simplicity.<sup id="cite_ref-Clayden668_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayden668-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most useful silicon-containing groups is <a href="/wiki/Trimethylsilyl" title="Trimethylsilyl">trimethylsilyl</a>, Me<sub>3</sub>Si–. The Si–C bond connecting it to the rest of the molecule is reasonably strong, allowing it to remain while the rest of the molecule undergoes reactions, but is not so strong that it cannot be removed specifically when needed, for example by the <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> ion, which is a very weak nucleophile for carbon compounds but a very strong one for organosilicon compounds. It may be compared to acidic <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>; while trisilylmethyl is removed by hard nucleophiles instead of bases, both removals usually promote elimination. As a general rule, while saturated carbon is best attacked by nucleophiles that are neutral compounds, those based on nonmetals far down on the periodic table (e.g. sulfur, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>), or even both, silicon is best attacked by charged nucleophiles, particularly those involving such highly electronegative nonmetals as oxygen, fluorine, or chlorine. For example, enolates react at the carbon in <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">haloalkanes</a>, but at the oxygen in <a href="/wiki/Silyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Silyl">silyl</a> chlorides; and when trimethylsilyl is removed from an organic molecule using hydroxide as a nucleophile, the product of the reaction is not the silanol as one would expect from using carbon chemistry as an analogy, because the siloxide is strongly nucleophilic and attacks the original molecule to yield the <a href="/wiki/Silyl_ether" title="Silyl ether">silyl ether</a> <a href="/wiki/Hexamethyldisiloxane" title="Hexamethyldisiloxane">hexamethyldisiloxane</a>, (Me<sub>3</sub>Si)<sub>2</sub>O. Conversely, while the S<sub>N</sub>2 reaction is mostly unaffected by the presence of a partial positive charge (δ+) at the carbon, the analogous "S<sub>N</sub>2" reaction at silicon is so affected. Thus, for example, the silyl <a href="/wiki/Triflate" title="Triflate">triflates</a> are so electrophilic that they react 10<sup>8</sup> to 10<sup>9</sup> times faster than silyl chlorides with oxygen-containing nucleophiles. <a href="/wiki/Trimethylsilyl_triflate" class="mw-redirect" title="Trimethylsilyl triflate">Trimethylsilyl triflate</a> is in particular a very good <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> and is used to convert <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonyl">carbonyl</a> compounds to <a href="/wiki/Acetal" title="Acetal">acetals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silyl_enol_ether" title="Silyl enol ether">silyl enol ethers</a>, reacting them together analogously to the <a href="/wiki/Aldol_reaction" title="Aldol reaction">aldol reaction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clayden668_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayden668-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Si–C bonds are commonly formed in three ways. In the laboratory, preparation is often carried out in small quantities by reacting tetrachlorosilane with <a href="/wiki/Organolithium" class="mw-redirect" title="Organolithium">organolithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" class="mw-redirect" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Organoaluminium" class="mw-redirect" title="Organoaluminium">organoaluminium</a> reagents, or by catalytic addition of Si–H across C=C double bonds. The second route has the drawback of not being applicable to the most important silanes, the methyl and phenyl silanes. Organosilanes are made industrially by directly reacting alkyl or aryl halides with silicon with 10% by weight metallic <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> as a catalyst. Standard organic reactions suffice to produce many derivatives; the resulting organosilanes are often significantly more reactive than their carbon congeners, readily undergoing hydrolysis, ammonolysis, alcoholysis, and condensation to form cyclic oligomers or linear polymers.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicone_polymers">Silicone polymers</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">Silicone</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PmdsStructure.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/220px-PmdsStructure.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/330px-PmdsStructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/440px-PmdsStructure.png 2x" data-file-width="1217" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PmdsStructure.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Polydimethylsiloxane" title="Polydimethylsiloxane">polydimethylsiloxane</a>, the principal component of silicones</div></div></div>
<p>The word "silicone" was first used by Frederic Kipping in 1901. He invented the word to illustrate the similarity of chemical formulae between Ph<sub>2</sub>SiO and <a href="/wiki/Benzophenone" title="Benzophenone">benzophenone</a>, Ph<sub>2</sub>CO, although he also stressed the lack of chemical resemblance due to the polymeric structure of Ph<sub>2</sub>SiO, which is not shared by Ph<sub>2</sub>CO.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicones may be considered analogous to mineral silicates, in which the methyl groups of the silicones correspond to the <a href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" class="mw-redirect" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> O<sup>−</sup> of the silicates.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> They are quite stable to extreme temperatures, oxidation, and water, and have useful dielectric, antistick, and antifoam properties. Furthermore, they are resistant over long periods of time to ultraviolet radiation and weathering, and are inert physiologically. They are fairly unreactive, but do react with concentrated solutions bearing the hydroxide ion and fluorinating agents, and occasionally can be even used as mild reagents for selective syntheses. For example, (Me<sub>3</sub>Si)<sub>2</sub>O is valuable for the preparation of derivatives of <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> oxyhalides, converting a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_hexachloride" title="Tungsten hexachloride">tungsten hexachloride</a> suspension in <a href="/wiki/Dichloroethane" title="Dichloroethane">dichloroethane</a> solution quantitatively to WOCl<sub>4</sub> in under an hour at room temperature, and then to yellow WO<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub> at 100&#160;°C in light petroleum at a yield of 95% overnight.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood361_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood361-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg/220px-Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="327" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Olivine</div></div></div>
<p>In the universe, silicon is the seventh most abundant element, coming after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>. These abundances are not replicated well on Earth due to substantial separation of the elements taking place during the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Silicon makes up 27.2% of the Earth's crust by weight, second only to oxygen at 45.5%, with which it is always associated in nature. Further fractionation took place in the formation of the Earth by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">planetary differentiation</a>: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_core" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s core">Earth's core</a>, which makes up 31.5% of the mass of the Earth, has approximate composition Fe<sub>25</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>S<sub>3</sub>; the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> makes up 68.1% of the Earth's mass and is composed mostly of denser oxides and silicates, an example being <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, (Mg,Fe)<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>; while the lighter siliceous minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminosilicate" title="Aluminosilicate">aluminosilicates</a> rise to the surface and form the crust, making up 0.4% of the Earth's mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood329_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood329-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The crystallisation of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a> from magma depends on a number of factors; among them are the chemical composition of the magma, the cooling rate, and some properties of the individual minerals to be formed, such as <a href="/wiki/Lattice_energy" title="Lattice energy">lattice energy</a>, melting point, and complexity of their crystal structure. As magma is cooled, <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> appears first, followed by <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibole" title="Amphibole">amphibole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biotite" title="Biotite">biotite</a> mica, <a href="/wiki/Orthoclase_feldspar" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthoclase feldspar">orthoclase feldspar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscovite_mica" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscovite mica">muscovite mica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>, and finally hydrothermal minerals. This sequence shows a trend towards increasingly complex silicate units with cooling, and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> anions in addition to oxides. Many metals can substitute for silicon. After these igneous rocks undergo <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, transport, and deposition, <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> like clay, shale, and sandstone are formed. <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">Metamorphism</a> also can occur at high temperatures and pressures, creating an even vaster variety of minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood329_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood329-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<p>Silicon of 96–99% purity is made by reducing <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a> or sand with highly pure <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>. The reduction is carried out in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>, with an excess of SiO<sub>2</sub> used to stop <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> (SiC) from accumulating:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiO<sub>2</sub> + 2 C → Si + 2 CO</dd>
<dd>2 SiC + SiO<sub>2</sub> → 3 Si + 2 CO</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ferrosilicon.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/200px-Ferrosilicon.JPG" width="200" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/300px-Ferrosilicon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/400px-Ferrosilicon.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1755" data-file-height="1227" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ferrosilicon.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ferrosilicon alloy</div></div></div>
<p>This reaction, known as carbothermal reduction of silicon dioxide, is usually conducted in the presence of scrap iron with low amounts of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Ferrosilicon, an iron-silicon alloy that contains varying ratios of elemental silicon and iron, accounts for about 80% of the world's production of elemental silicon, with China, the leading supplier of elemental silicon, providing 4.6 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (or 2/3 of world output) of silicon, most of it in the form of ferrosilicon. It is followed by Russia (610,000 t), Norway (330,000 t), Brazil (240,000 t) and the United States (170,000 t).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Ferrosilicon is primarily used by the iron and steel industry (see below) with primary use as alloying addition in iron or steel and for de-oxidation of steel in integrated steel plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Another sometimes used reaction is aluminothermal reduction of silicon dioxide, as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann574_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann574-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 SiO<sub>2</sub> + 4 Al → 3 Si + 2 Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Leaching powdered 96–97% pure silicon with water results in ~98.5% pure silicon, which is used in the chemical industry. However, even greater purity is needed for semiconductor applications, and this is produced from the reduction of tetrachlorosilane or trichlorosilane. The former is made by chlorinating scrap silicon and the latter is a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> production. These compounds are volatile and hence can be purified by repeated <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>, followed by reduction to elemental silicon with very pure <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> metal as the reducing agent. The spongy pieces of silicon thus produced are melted and then grown to form cylindrical single crystals, before being purified by <a href="/wiki/Zone_refining" class="mw-redirect" title="Zone refining">zone refining</a>. Other routes use the thermal decomposition of silane or tetraiodosilane. Another process used is the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hexafluorosilicate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium hexafluorosilicate">sodium hexafluorosilicate</a>, a common waste product of the phosphate fertilizer industry, by metallic <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>: this is highly exothermic and hence requires no outside fuel source. Hyperfine silicon is made at a higher purity than almost every other material: <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> production requires impurity levels in silicon crystals less than 1 part per 10<sup>10</sup>, and in special cases impurity levels below 1 part per 10<sup>12</sup> are needed and attained.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood330_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood330-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<p>Most silicon is used industrially without being purified, and indeed often with comparatively little processing from its natural form. Over 90% of the Earth's crust is composed of <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>, which are compounds of silicon and oxygen, often with metallic ions when negatively charged silicate anions require cations to balance the charge. Many of these have direct commercial uses, such as clays, <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a> sand and most kinds of building stone. Thus, the vast majority of uses for silicon are as structural compounds, either as the silicate minerals or silica (crude silicon dioxide). Silicates are used in making <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> (made mostly of calcium silicates) which is used in <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">building mortar</a> and modern <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a>, but more importantly, combined with silica sand, and gravel (usually containing silicate minerals like granite), to make the <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> that is the basis of most of the very largest industrial building projects of the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood356_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood356-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silica is used to make <a href="/wiki/Fire_brick" title="Fire brick">fire brick</a>, a type of ceramic. Silicate minerals are also in whiteware <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, an important class of products usually containing various types of fired <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> minerals (natural aluminium phyllosilicates). An example is <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> which is based on the silicate mineral <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a>. Traditional <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> (silica-based <a href="/wiki/Soda-lime_glass" class="mw-redirect" title="Soda-lime glass">soda-lime glass</a>) also functions in many of the same ways, and is also used for windows and containers. In addition, specialty silica based <a href="/wiki/Glass_fiber" title="Glass fiber">glass fibers</a> are used for <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a>, as well as to produce <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a> for structural support and <a href="/wiki/Glass_wool" title="Glass wool">glass wool</a> for thermal insulation.
</p><p>Silicones are often used in <a href="/wiki/Waterproofing" title="Waterproofing">waterproofing</a> treatments, <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">molding</a> compounds, mold-<a href="/wiki/Release_agent" title="Release agent">release agents</a>, mechanical seals, high temperature <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">greases</a> and waxes, and <a href="/wiki/Caulking" class="mw-redirect" title="Caulking">caulking</a> compounds. Silicone is also sometimes used in <a href="/wiki/Breast_implant" title="Breast implant">breast implants</a>, contact lenses, <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silly_Putty" title="Silly Putty">Silly Putty</a> was originally made by adding <a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">boric acid</a> to <a href="/wiki/Silicone_oil" title="Silicone oil">silicone oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Other silicon compounds function as high-technology abrasives and new high-strength ceramics based upon <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a>. Silicon is a component of some <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">superalloys</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span></h3>
<p>Elemental silicon is added to molten <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> as <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a> or silicocalcium alloys to improve performance in casting thin sections and to prevent the formation of <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a> where exposed to outside air. The presence of elemental silicon in molten iron acts as a sink for oxygen, so that the steel carbon content, which must be kept within narrow limits for each type of steel, can be more closely controlled. Ferrosilicon production and use is a monitor of the steel industry, and although this form of elemental silicon is grossly impure, it accounts for 80% of the world's use of free silicon. Silicon is an important constituent of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_steel" title="Electrical steel">electrical steel</a>, modifying its <a href="/wiki/Resistivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> properties.
</p><p>The properties of silicon can be used to modify alloys with metals other than iron. "Metallurgical grade" silicon is silicon of 95–99% purity. About 55% of the world consumption of metallurgical purity silicon goes for production of aluminium-silicon alloys (<a href="/wiki/Silumin" title="Silumin">silumin</a> alloys) for aluminium part <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">casts</a>, mainly for use in the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a>. Silicon's importance in aluminium casting is that a significantly high amount (12%) of silicon in aluminium forms a <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" class="mw-redirect" title="Eutectic mixture">eutectic mixture</a> which solidifies with very little thermal contraction. This greatly reduces tearing and cracks formed from stress as casting alloys cool to solidity. Silicon also significantly improves the hardness and thus wear-resistance of aluminium.<sup id="cite_ref-diecasting_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diecasting-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGS_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">Electronics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device_fabrication" title="Semiconductor device fabrication">Semiconductor device fabrication</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg/220px-Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="357" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon wafer with mirror finish</div></div></div>
<p>Most elemental silicon produced remains as a ferrosilicon alloy, and only about 20% is refined to metallurgical grade purity (a total of 1.3–1.5 million metric tons/year). An estimated 15% of the world production of metallurgical grade silicon is further refined to semiconductor purity.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> This typically is the "nine-9" or 99.9999999% purity,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> nearly defect-free single <a href="/wiki/Crystalline" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystalline">crystalline</a> material.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline_silicon" title="Monocrystalline silicon">Monocrystalline silicon</a> of such purity is usually produced by the <a href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">Czochralski process</a>, is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Wafer_(electronics)" title="Wafer (electronics)">silicon wafers</a> used in the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_industry" title="Semiconductor industry">semiconductor industry</a>, in electronics, and in some high-cost and high-efficiency <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann590_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann590-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Pure silicon is an <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_semiconductor" title="Intrinsic semiconductor">intrinsic semiconductor</a>, which means that unlike metals, it conducts <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">electron holes</a> and electrons released from atoms by heat; silicon's <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> increases with higher temperatures. Pure silicon has too low a conductivity (i.e., too high a <a href="/wiki/Resistivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a>) to be used as a circuit element in electronics. In practice, pure silicon is <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductors)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doping (semiconductors)">doped</a> with small concentrations of certain other elements, which greatly increase its conductivity and adjust its electrical response by controlling the number and charge (<a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">positive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">negative</a>) of activated carriers. Such control is necessary for <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_detector" title="Semiconductor detector">semiconductor detectors</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">semiconductor devices</a> used in the computer industry and other technical applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann573_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann573-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Silicon_photonics" title="Silicon photonics">silicon photonics</a>, silicon can be used as a continuous wave <a href="/wiki/Raman_laser" title="Raman laser">Raman laser</a> medium to produce coherent light.<sup id="cite_ref-dekker_2008_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dekker_2008-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In common <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuits</a>, a wafer of monocrystalline silicon serves as a mechanical support for the circuits, which are created by doping and insulated from each other by thin layers of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon oxide</a>, an insulator that is easily produced on Si surfaces by processes of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thermal_Oxidation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thermal Oxidation (page does not exist)">Thermal Oxidation</a> or <a href="/wiki/LOCOS" title="LOCOS">Local Oxidation (LOCOS)</a>, which involve exposing the element to oxygen under the proper conditions that can be predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Deal%E2%80%93Grove_model" title="Deal–Grove model">Deal–Grove model</a>. Silicon has become the most popular material for both high power semiconductors and integrated circuits because it can withstand the highest temperatures and greatest electrical activity without suffering <a href="/wiki/Avalanche_breakdown" title="Avalanche breakdown">avalanche breakdown</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Electron_avalanche" title="Electron avalanche">electron avalanche</a> is created when heat produces free electrons and holes, which in turn pass more current, which produces more heat). In addition, the insulating oxide of silicon is not soluble in water, which gives it an advantage over <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> (an element with similar properties which can also be used in semiconductor devices) in certain fabrication techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Monocrystalline silicon is expensive to produce, and is usually justified only in production of integrated circuits, where tiny crystal imperfections can interfere with tiny circuit paths. For other uses, other types of pure silicon may be employed. These include <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenated_amorphous_silicon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogenated amorphous silicon">hydrogenated amorphous silicon</a> and upgraded metallurgical-grade silicon (UMG-Si) used in the production of low-cost, <a href="/wiki/Large-area_electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Large-area electronics">large-area electronics</a> in applications such as <a href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid crystal display">liquid crystal displays</a> and of large-area, low-cost, thin-film <a href="/wiki/Solar_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar cells">solar cells</a>. Such semiconductor grades of silicon are either slightly less pure or polycrystalline rather than monocrystalline, and are produced in comparable quantities as the monocrystalline silicon: 75,000 to 150,000 metric tons per year.  The market for the lesser grade is growing more quickly than for monocrystalline silicon. By 2013, polycrystalline silicon production, used mostly in solar cells, was projected to reach 200,000 metric tons per year, while monocrystalline semiconductor grade silicon was expected to remain less than 50,000 tons/year.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/220px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/330px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/440px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diatom, enclosed in a silica cell wall</div></div></div>
<p>Although silicon is readily available in the form of <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>, very few organisms use it directly. <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">Diatoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiolaria" title="Radiolaria">radiolaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siliceous_sponge" title="Siliceous sponge">siliceous sponges</a> use <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_silica" title="Biogenic silica">biogenic silica</a> as a structural material for skeletons. In more advanced plants, the silica <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> (opal phytoliths) are rigid microscopic bodies occurring in the cell; some plants, for example <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, need silicon for their growth.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abd_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abd-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-plants_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plants-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is some evidence that silicon is important to nail, hair, bone and skin health in humans,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> for example in studies that show that premenopausal women with higher dietary silicon intake have higher <a href="/wiki/Bone_density" title="Bone density">bone density</a>, and that silicon supplementation can increase bone volume and density in patients with <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jugdaohsingh2007silicon_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jugdaohsingh2007silicon-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Silicon is needed for synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> contains the greatest quantity in the human body<sup id="cite_ref-LoeperLoeper1978_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LoeperLoeper1978-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and has been considered an <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">essential element</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Niels_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Niels-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> nevertheless, it is difficult to prove its essentiality, because silicon is very common and hence deficiency symptoms are difficult to reproduce.<sup id="cite_ref-lipp_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lipp-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon is currently under consideration for elevation to the status of a "plant beneficial substance by the Association of American Plant Food Control Officials (AAPFCO)."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-presentation_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Silicon has been shown to improve plant cell wall strength and structural integrity in some plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Kim2002_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2002-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<p>People can be exposed to elemental silicon in the workplace by breathing it in, swallowing it, skin contact, and eye contact. In the latter two cases, silicon poses a slight hazard as an irritant; it is hazardous if inhaled.<sup id="cite_ref-msds_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msds-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">Permissible exposure limit</a>) for silicon exposure in the workplace as 15&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> total exposure and 5&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> respiratory exposure over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">Recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 10&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> total exposure and 5&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> respiratory exposure over an 8-hour workday.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Inhalation of <a href="/wiki/Crystalline" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystalline">crystalline</a> silica dust may lead to <a href="/wiki/Silicosis" title="Silicosis">silicosis</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Occupational_lung_disease" title="Occupational lung disease">occupational lung disease</a> marked by <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> and scarring in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nodule_(medicine)" title="Nodule (medicine)">nodular lesions</a> in the upper lobes of the <a href="/wiki/Lungs" class="mw-redirect" title="Lungs">lungs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PlantVoulvoulis2012_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlantVoulvoulis2012-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

London: Edward Arnold. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7131-2639-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-7131-2639-6">0-7131-2639-6</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Plant+Anatomy.+Part+1+Cells+and+Tissues&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Edward+Arnold&amp;rft.date=1978&amp;rft.isbn=0-7131-2639-6&amp;rft.aulast=Cutter&amp;rft.aufirst=Elizabeth+G.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASilicon" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his table of the elements, Lavoisier listed five "salifiable earths" (i.e., ores that could be made to react with acids to produce salts (<i>salis</i> = salt, in Latin): <i>chaux</i> (calcium oxide), <i>magnésie</i> (magnesia, magnesium oxide), <i>baryte</i> (barium sulfate), <i>alumine</i> (alumina, aluminium oxide), and <i>silice</i> (silica, silicon dioxide). About these "elements", Lavoisier speculates: "We are probably only acquainted as yet with a part of the metallic substances existing in nature, as all those which have a stronger affinity to oxygen than carbon possesses, are incapable, hitherto, of being reduced to a metallic state, and consequently, being only presented to our observation under the form of oxyds, are confounded with earths. It is extremely probable that barytes, which we have just now arranged with earths, is in this situation; for in many experiments it exhibits properties nearly approaching to those of metallic bodies. It is even possible that all the substances we call earths may be only metallic oxyds, irreducible by any hitherto known process." – from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=adYKAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA218">page 218</a> of: Lavoisier with Robert Kerr, trans., <i>Elements of Chemistry</i>, … , 4th ed. (Edinburgh, Scotland: William Creech, 1799). (The original passage appears in: Lavoisier, <i>Traité Élémentaire de Chimie</i>, (Paris, France: Cuchet, 1789), vol. 1, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=hZch3yOrayUC&amp;pg=PA174">p. 174</a>.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Greenwood328-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Greenwood328_10-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Greenwood and Earnshaw, p. 328–329</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davy, Humphry (1808) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Kg9GAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA333">"Electro chemical researches, on the decomposition of the earths; with observations on the metals obtained from the alkaline earths, and on the amalgam procured from ammonia,"</a> <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society</i> [of London], <b>98</b>&#160;: 333–370. On <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Kg9GAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA353">p. 353</a> Davy coins the name "silicium"&#160;: "Had I been so fortunate as to have obtained more certain evidences on this subject, and to have procured the metallic substances I was in search of, I should have proposed for them the names of silicium [silicon], alumium [aluminium], zirconium, and glucium [beryllium]."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Thomson, <i>A System of Chemistry in Four Volumes</i>, 5th ed. (London, England: Baldwin, Cradock, and Joy, 1817), vol. 1. From <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=zVA0AQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA252">p. 252</a>: "The base of silica has been usually considered as a metal, and called <i>silicium</i>. But as there is not the smallest evidence for its metallic nature, and as it bears a close resemblance to boron and carbon, it is better to class it along with these bodies, and to give it the name of <i>silicon</i>."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See:
<ul><li>Berzelius announced his discovery of silicon ("silicium") in: Berzelius, J. (presented: 1823; published: 1824) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=pJlPAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA46">"Undersökning af flusspatssyran och dess märkvärdigaste föreningar"</a> (Investigation of hydrofluoric acid and of its most noteworthy compounds), <i>Kongliga Vetenskaps-Academiens Handlingar</i> [Proceedings of the Royal Science Academy], <b>12</b>&#160;: 46–98. The isolation of silicon and its characterization are detailed in the section titled "Flussspatssyrad kisseljords sönderdelning med kalium," pp. 46–68.</li>
<li>The above article was reprinted in German in: J. J. Berzelius (1824) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k15086x/f185.image.langEN">"<i>II. Untersuchungen über Flussspathsäure und deren merkwürdigsten Verbindungen</i>"</a> (II. Investigations of hydrofluoric acid and its most noteworthy compounds), <i>Annalen der Physik</i>, <b>77</b>: 169–230. The isolation of silicon is detailed in the section titled: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k15086x/f220.image.langEN">"Zersetzung der flussspaths. Kieselerde durch Kalium"</a> (Decomposition of silicate fluoride by potassium), pp. 204–210.</li>
<li>The above article was reprinted in French in: Berzelius (1824) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=eHmstO7CmqAC&amp;pg=PA337">"Décomposition du fluate de silice par le potassium"</a> (Decomposition of silica fluoride by potassium), <i>Annales de Chimie et de Physique</i>, <b>27</b>: 337–359.</li>
<li>Reprinted in English in: Berzelius (1825) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=_UwwAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA254">"On the mode of obtaining silicium, and on the characters and properties of that substance,"</a> <i>Philosophical Magazine</i>, <b>65</b> (324): 254–267.</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal"><a href="/wiki/Mary_Elvira_Weeks" title="Mary Elvira Weeks">Weeks, Mary Elvira</a> (1932). "The discovery of the elements: XII. Other elements isolated with the aid of potassium and sodium: beryllium, boron, silicon, and aluminum". <i>Journal of Chemical Education</i>. <b>9</b> (8): 1386–1412. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1932JChEd...9.1386W">1932JChEd...9.1386W</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1021%2Fed009p1386">10.1021/ed009p1386</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Chemical+Education&amp;rft.atitle=The+discovery+of+the+elements%3A+XII.+Other+elements+isolated+with+the+aid+of+potassium+and+sodium%3A+beryllium%2C+boron%2C+silicon%2C+and+aluminum&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=8&amp;rft.pages=1386-1412&amp;rft.date=1932&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1021%2Fed009p1386&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1932JChEd...9.1386W&amp;rft.aulast=Weeks&amp;rft.aufirst=Mary+Elvira&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASilicon" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Voronkov, M. G. (2007). "Silicon era". <i>Russian Journal of Applied Chemistry</i>. <b>80</b> (12): 2190. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1134%2FS1070427207120397">10.1134/S1070427207120397</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Russian+Journal+of+Applied+Chemistry&amp;rft.atitle=Silicon+era&amp;rft.volume=80&amp;rft.issue=12&amp;rft.pages=2190&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1134%2FS1070427207120397&amp;rft.aulast=Voronkov&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+G.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASilicon" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1854, Deville was trying to prepare aluminium metal from aluminium chloride that was heavily contaminated with silicon chloride. Deville used two methods to prepare aluminium: heating aluminium chloride with sodium metal in an inert atmosphere (of hydrogen); and melting aluminum chloride with sodium chloride and then electrolyzing the mixture. In both cases, pure silicon was produced: the silicon dissolved in the molten aluminium, but crystallized upon cooling. Dissolving the crude aluminum in hydrochloric acid revealed flakes of crystallized silicon. See: Henri Sainte-Claire Deville (1854) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=C3VFAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA321">"Note sur deux procédés de préparation de l'aluminium et sur une nouvelle forme du silicium"</a> (Note on two procedures for the preparation of aluminium and on a new form of silicon), <i>Comptes rendus</i>, <b>39</b>: 321–326.<br />Subsequently, Deville obtained crystalline silicon by heating the chloride or fluoride of silicon with sodium metal, isolating the amorphous silicon, then melting the amorphous form with salt and heating the mixture until most of the salt evaporated. See: H. Sainte-Claire Deville (1855) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=tZhDAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA1034">"Du silicium et du titane"</a> (On silicon and titanium), <i>Comptes rendus</i>, <b>40</b>: 1034–1036.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://elements.etacude.com/Si.php">Information on silicon – history, </a><a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermodynamic">thermodynamic</a>, chemical, physical and electronic properties: Etacude.com. Elements.etacude.com. Retrieved on 2011-08-07.</span>
</li>

Nautilus.fis.uc.pt. Retrieved on 2011-08-07.</span>
